      <p>
       The
       <b>
        Gregorian calendar
       </b>
       is internationally the most widely used
       <a href="/wiki/Civil_calendar" title="Civil calendar">
        civil calendar
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-1">
         [1]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-2">
         [2]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-3">
         [Note 1]
        </a>
       </sup>
       It is named after
       <a href="/wiki/Pope_Gregory_XIII" title="Pope Gregory XIII">
        Pope Gregory XIII
       </a>
       , who introduced it in October 1582.
      </p>
      <p>
       It was a refinement to the
       <a href="/wiki/Julian_calendar" title="Julian calendar">
        Julian calendar
       </a>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-text_4-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-text-4">
         [3]
        </a>
       </sup>
       involving an approximately 0.002% correction in the length of the calendar
       <a href="/wiki/Year" title="Year">
        year
       </a>
       . The motivation for the reform was to stop the drift of the calendar with respect to the equinoxes and solstices—particularly the northern
       <a href="/wiki/March_equinox" title="March equinox">
        vernal equinox
       </a>
       , which
       <a href="/wiki/Computus" title="Computus">
        helps set the date for Easter
       </a>
       . Transition to the Gregorian calendar would restore the holiday to the time of the year in which it was celebrated when introduced by the early Church. The reform was adopted initially by the Catholic countries of Europe.
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Protestant_Reformation" title="Protestant Reformation">
        Protestants
       </a>
       and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eastern_Orthodoxy" title="Eastern Orthodoxy">
        Eastern Orthodox
       </a>
       countries continued to use the traditional Julian calendar and adopted the Gregorian reform, one by one, after a time, at least for civil purposes and for the sake of convenience in international trade. The last European country to adopt the reform was Greece, in 1923. Many (but not all) countries that have traditionally used the Julian calendar, or the
       <a href="/wiki/Islamic_calendar" title="Islamic calendar">
        Islamic
       </a>
       or other religious calendars, have come to adopt the Gregorian calendar for civil purposes.
      </p>
      <p>
       The Gregorian reform contained two parts: a reform of the Julian calendar as used prior to
       <a href="/wiki/Pope_Gregory_XIII" title="Pope Gregory XIII">
        Pope Gregory XIII
       </a>
       's time, and a reform of the lunar cycle used by the Church with the Julian calendar to calculate the date of Easter. The reform was a modification of a proposal made by
       <a href="/wiki/Aloysius_Lilius" title="Aloysius Lilius">
        Aloysius Lilius
       </a>
       ,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Moyer_1983_5-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Moyer_1983-5">
         [4]
        </a>
       </sup>
       who proposed to reduce the number of
       <a href="/wiki/Leap_year" title="Leap year">
        leap years
       </a>
       that occur in every four centuries from 100 to 97, by making 3 out of 4 centurial years common years instead of leap years. Lilius also produced an original and practical scheme for adjusting the
       <a href="/wiki/Epact" title="Epact">
        epacts
       </a>
       of the moon when calculating the annual date of Easter, solving a long-standing obstacle to calendar reform.
      </p>
      <p>
       The Gregorian reform modified the Julian calendar's scheme of leap years as follows:
      </p>
      <blockquote>
       <p>
        Every year that is exactly divisible by four is a leap year, except for years that are exactly divisible by 100, but these centurial years are leap years if they are exactly divisible by 400. For example, the years 1700, 1800, and 1900 are not leap years, but the year 2000 is.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6">
         <a href="#cite_note-6">
          [5]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </p>
      </blockquote>
      <p>
       In addition to the change in the mean length of the calendar year from 365.25 days (365 days 6 hours) to 365.2425 days (365 days 5 hours 49 minutes 12 seconds), a reduction of 10 minutes 48 seconds per year, the Gregorian calendar reform also dealt with the accumulated difference between these lengths. The canonical Easter tables were devised at the end of the third century, when the vernal equinox fell either on 20 March or 21 March depending on the year's position in the leap year cycle. As the rule was that the full moon preceding Easter was not to precede the equinox, the date was fixed at 21 March for computational purposes and the earliest date for Easter was fixed at 22 March. The Gregorian calendar reproduced these conditions by removing ten days.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7">
        <a href="#cite_note-7">
         [6]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       To unambiguously specify a date,
       <a href="/wiki/Dual_dating" title="Dual dating">
        dual dating
       </a>
       or
       <a href="/wiki/Old_Style_and_New_Style_dates" title="Old Style and New Style dates">
        Old Style and New Style dates
       </a>
       are sometimes used. Dual dating gives two consecutive years for a given date because of differences in the starting date of the year or to give both the Julian and the Gregorian dates. The "Old Style" (O.S.) and "New Style" (N.S.) notations indicate either that the start of the Julian year has (or has not) been adjusted to start on 1 January (even though documents written at the time use a different start of year), or that a date conforms to the (old) Julian calendar rather than the (new) Gregorian.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8">
        <a href="#cite_note-8">
         [Note 2]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The Gregorian calendar continued to use the previous
       <a href="/wiki/Calendar_era" title="Calendar era">
        calendar era
       </a>
       (year-numbering system), which counts years from the traditional date of
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Birth_of_Jesus" title="Birth of Jesus">
        the nativity
       </a>
       (
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Anno_Domini" title="Anno Domini">
         Anno Domini
        </a>
       </i>
       ), originally calculated in the 6th century by
       <a href="/wiki/Dionysius_Exiguus" title="Dionysius Exiguus">
        Dionysius Exiguus
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9">
        <a href="#cite_note-9">
         [7]
        </a>
       </sup>
       This year-numbering system, also known as Dionysian era or
       <a href="/wiki/Common_Era" title="Common Era">
        Common Era
       </a>
       , is the predominant international standard today.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11">
        <a href="#cite_note-11">
         [Note 3]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
      </p>

      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Description">
        Description
       </span>
      </h2>
      <table class="wikitable" style="float: right">
       <caption>
        A year is divided into twelve
        <a href="/wiki/Month" title="Month">
         months
        </a>
       </caption>

      <ul class="gallery mw-gallery-traditional">
       <li class="gallerybox" style="width: 155px">
        <div style="width: 155px">
         <div class="thumb" style="width: 150px;">
          <div style="margin:15px auto;">
           <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Christopher_Clavius.jpg">
            <img alt="" data-file-height="1406" data-file-width="972" height="120" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/Christopher_Clavius.jpg/83px-Christopher_Clavius.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/Christopher_Clavius.jpg/124px-Christopher_Clavius.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/Christopher_Clavius.jpg/166px-Christopher_Clavius.jpg 2x" width="83"/>
           </a>
          </div>
         </div>
         <div class="gallerytext">
          <p>
           <a href="/wiki/Christopher_Clavius" title="Christopher Clavius">
            Christopher Clavius
           </a>
           (1538–1612), one of the main authors of the reform.
          </p>
         </div>
        </div>
       </li>
       <li class="gallerybox" style="width: 155px">
        <div style="width: 155px">
         <div class="thumb" style="width: 150px;">
          <div style="margin:15px auto;">
           <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Pope_Gregory_XIII.jpg">
            <img alt="" data-file-height="1546" data-file-width="1000" height="120" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/Pope_Gregory_XIII.jpg/77px-Pope_Gregory_XIII.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/Pope_Gregory_XIII.jpg/116px-Pope_Gregory_XIII.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/Pope_Gregory_XIII.jpg/155px-Pope_Gregory_XIII.jpg 2x" width="77"/>
           </a>
          </div>
         </div>
         <div class="gallerytext">
          <p>
           <a href="/wiki/Pope_Gregory_XIII" title="Pope Gregory XIII">
            Pope Gregory XIII
           </a>
           in an early 17th-century engraving.
          </p>
         </div>
        </div>
       </li>
      </ul>
      <p>
       The Gregorian calendar is a
       <a href="/wiki/Solar_calendar" title="Solar calendar">
        solar calendar
       </a>
       . A regular Gregorian year consists of 365 days, but as in the Julian calendar, in certain years, a
       <a href="/wiki/Leap_year" title="Leap year">
        leap year
       </a>
       , a
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Leap_day" title="Leap day">
        leap day
       </a>
       is added to February. In the Julian calendar a leap year occurs every 4 years, but the Gregorian calendar omits a leap day in three of every 400 years. In the Julian calendar, the leap day was inserted by doubling 24 February, and the Gregorian reform did not change the date of the leap day. In the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Modern_period" title="Modern period">
        modern period
       </a>
       , it has become customary to number the days from the beginning of the month, and 29 February is typically considered as the leap day. Some churches, notably the Roman Catholic Church, delay February festivals after the 23rd by one day in leap years.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12">
        <a href="#cite_note-12">
         [9]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Gregorian years are identified by consecutive year numbers.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13">
        <a href="#cite_note-13">
         [10]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The cycles repeat completely every 146,097 days, which equals 400 years.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14">
        <a href="#cite_note-14">
         [Note 4]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16">
        <a href="#cite_note-16">
         [Note 5]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Of these 400 years, 303 are regular years of 365 days and 97 are leap years of 366 days. A mean calendar year is 365
       <span class="mwe-math-element">
        <span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">
         <math alttext="{\displaystyle {\tfrac {97}{400}}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
          <semantics>
           <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
            <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
             <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
              <mstyle displaystyle="false" scriptlevel="0">
               <mfrac>
                <mn>
                 97
                </mn>
                <mn>
                 400
                </mn>
               </mfrac>
              </mstyle>
             </mrow>
            </mstyle>
           </mrow>
           <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">
            {\displaystyle {\tfrac {97}{400}}}
           </annotation>
          </semantics>
         </math>
        </span>
        <img alt="{\tfrac {97}{400}}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/2d157a66179e3fb694ccc7412417f796b3bb4dc0" style="vertical-align: -1.338ex; width:3.302ex; height:3.843ex;"/>
       </span>
       days = 365.2425 days, or 365 days, 5 hours, 49 minutes and 12 seconds.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17">
        <a href="#cite_note-17">
         [Note 6]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       A calendar date is fully specified by the year (numbered by some scheme beyond the scope of the calendar itself), the month (identified by name or number), and the day of the month (numbered sequentially starting at 1). Although the calendar year currently runs from 1 January to 31 December, at previous times year numbers were based on a different starting point within the calendar (see the
       <a href="#Beginning_of_the_year">
        "beginning of the year"
       </a>
       section below).
      </p>
      <div style="clear:both;">
      </div>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Gregorian_reform">
        Gregorian reform
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Inter-grav.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="914" data-file-width="596" height="337" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Inter-grav.jpg/220px-Inter-grav.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Inter-grav.jpg/330px-Inter-grav.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Inter-grav.jpg/440px-Inter-grav.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Inter-grav.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         First page of the papal bull
         <i>
          <a href="/wiki/Inter_gravissimas" title="Inter gravissimas">
           Inter gravissimas
          </a>
         </i>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Gregorianscher_Kalender_Petersdom.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="472" data-file-width="800" height="130" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/Gregorianscher_Kalender_Petersdom.jpg/220px-Gregorianscher_Kalender_Petersdom.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/Gregorianscher_Kalender_Petersdom.jpg/330px-Gregorianscher_Kalender_Petersdom.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/Gregorianscher_Kalender_Petersdom.jpg/440px-Gregorianscher_Kalender_Petersdom.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Gregorianscher_Kalender_Petersdom.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Detail of the pope's tomb by
         <a href="/wiki/Camillo_Rusconi" title="Camillo Rusconi">
          Camillo Rusconi
         </a>
         (completed 1723); Antonio Lilio is genuflecting before the pope, presenting his printed calendar.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       The Gregorian calendar was a reform of the Julian calendar. It was instituted in 1582 by Pope Gregory XIII, after whom the calendar was named, by
       <a href="/wiki/Papal_bull" title="Papal bull">
        papal bull
       </a>
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Inter_gravissimas" title="Inter gravissimas">
         Inter gravissimas
        </a>
       </i>
       dated 24 February 1582.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-text_4-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-text-4">
         [3]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The motivation for the adjustment was to bring the date for the celebration of Easter to the time of year in which it was celebrated when it was introduced by the early Church. The error in the Julian calendar (its assumption that there are exactly 365.25 days in a year) had led to the date of the equinox according to the calendar drifting from the observed reality, and thus an error had been introduced into the
       <a href="/wiki/Computus" title="Computus">
        calculation of the date of Easter
       </a>
       . Although a recommendation of the
       <a href="/wiki/First_Council_of_Nicaea" title="First Council of Nicaea">
        First Council of Nicaea
       </a>
       in 325 specified that all Christians should celebrate Easter on the same day, it took almost five centuries before virtually all Christians achieved that objective by adopting the rules of the Church of Alexandria (see
       <a href="/wiki/Easter" title="Easter">
        Easter
       </a>
       for the issues which arose).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18">
        <a href="#cite_note-18">
         [Note 7]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Background">
        Background
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Further information:
       <a href="/wiki/Computus" title="Computus">
        Computus
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       Because the date of Easter was tied to the Spring Equinox, the Roman Catholic Church considered the seasonal drift in the date of
       <a href="/wiki/Easter" title="Easter">
        Easter
       </a>
       undesirable. The Church of Alexandria celebrated Easter on the Sunday after the 14th day of the moon (computed using the
       <a href="/wiki/Metonic_cycle" title="Metonic cycle">
        Metonic cycle
       </a>
       ) that falls on or after the vernal equinox, which they placed on 21 March. However, the Church of Rome still regarded 25 March (Lady Day) as the equinox (until 342), and used a different cycle to compute the day of the moon.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19">
        <a href="#cite_note-19">
         [12]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In the Alexandrian system, since the 14th day of the Easter moon could fall at earliest on 21 March its first day could fall no earlier than 8 March and no later than 5 April. This meant that Easter varied between 22 March and 25 April. In Rome, Easter was not allowed to fall later than 21 April, that being the day of the
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Parilia" title="Parilia">
         Parilia
        </a>
       </i>
       or birthday of Rome and a pagan festival. The first day of the Easter moon could fall no earlier than 5 March and no later than 2 April.
      </p>
      <p>
       Easter was the Sunday after the 15th day of this moon, whose 14th day was allowed to precede the equinox. Where the two systems produced different dates there was generally a compromise so that both churches were able to celebrate on the same day. By the 10th century all churches (except some on the eastern border of the
       <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_Empire" title="Byzantine Empire">
        Byzantine Empire
       </a>
       ) had adopted the Alexandrian Easter, which still placed the vernal equinox on 21 March, although
       <a href="/wiki/Bede" title="Bede">
        Bede
       </a>
       had already noted its drift in 725—it had drifted even further by the 16th century.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20">
        <a href="#cite_note-20">
         [13]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Worse, the reckoned Moon that was used to
       <a href="/wiki/Computus" title="Computus">
        compute Easter
       </a>
       was fixed to the Julian year by a
       <a href="/wiki/Metonic_cycle" title="Metonic cycle">
        19-year cycle
       </a>
       . That approximation built up an error of one day every 310 years, so by the 16th century the
       <a href="/wiki/Lunar_calendar" title="Lunar calendar">
        lunar calendar
       </a>
       was out of phase with the real Moon by four days.
      </p>
      <p>
       European scholars had been well aware of the calendar drift since the early medieval period.
       <a href="/wiki/Bede" title="Bede">
        Bede
       </a>
       , writing in the 8th century, showed that the accumulated error in his day was more than three days.
       <a href="/wiki/Roger_Bacon" title="Roger Bacon">
        Roger Bacon
       </a>
       in c. 1200 estimated the error at seven or eight days.
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dante" title="Dante">
        Dante
       </a>
       , writing c. 1300, was aware of the need of a calendar reform. The first attempt to go forward with such a reform was undertaken by
       <a href="/wiki/Pope_Sixtus_IV" title="Pope Sixtus IV">
        Pope Sixtus IV
       </a>
       , who in 1475 invited
       <a href="/wiki/Regiomontanus" title="Regiomontanus">
        Regiomontanus
       </a>
       to the Vatican for this purpose. However, the project was interrupted by the death of Regiomontanus shortly after his arrival in Rome.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21">
        <a href="#cite_note-21">
         [14]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The increase of astronomical knowledge and the precision of observations towards the end of the 15th century made the question more pressing. Numerous publications over the following decades called for a calendar reform, among them a paper sent to the Vatican by the
       <a href="/wiki/University_of_Salamanca" title="University of Salamanca">
        University of Salamanca
       </a>
       in 1515,
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
          <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2017)">
           citation needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
       but the project was not taken up again until the 1540s, and implemented only under
       <a href="/wiki/Pope_Gregory_XIII" title="Pope Gregory XIII">
        Pope Gregory XIII
       </a>
       (r. 1572–1585).
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Preparation">
        Preparation
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       In 1545, the
       <a href="/wiki/Council_of_Trent" title="Council of Trent">
        Council of Trent
       </a>
       authorized
       <a href="/wiki/Pope_Paul_III" title="Pope Paul III">
        Pope Paul III
       </a>
       to reform the calendar, requiring that the date of the vernal equinox be restored to that which it held at the time of the First Council of Nicaea in 325 and that an alteration to the calendar be designed to prevent future drift. This would allow for a more consistent and accurate scheduling of the feast of Easter.
      </p>
      <p>
       In 1577, a
       <i>
        Compendium
       </i>
       was sent to expert mathematicians outside the reform commission for comments. Some of these experts, including
       <a href="/wiki/Giambattista_Benedetti" title="Giambattista Benedetti">
        Giambattista Benedetti
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Giuseppe_Moletti" title="Giuseppe Moletti">
        Giuseppe Moleto
       </a>
       , believed Easter should be computed from the true motions of the sun and moon, rather than using a tabular method, but these recommendations were not adopted.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22">
        <a href="#cite_note-22">
         [15]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The reform adopted was a modification of a proposal made by the
       <a href="/wiki/Calabria" title="Calabria">
        Calabrian
       </a>
       doctor
       <a href="/wiki/Aloysius_Lilius" title="Aloysius Lilius">
        Aloysius Lilius
       </a>
       (or Lilio).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Moyer_1983_5-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Moyer_1983-5">
         [4]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Lilius's proposal included reducing the number of leap years in four centuries from 100 to 97, by making three out of four centurial years common instead of leap years. He also produced an original and practical scheme for adjusting the epacts of the moon when calculating the annual date of Easter, solving a long-standing obstacle to calendar reform.
      </p>
      <p>
       Ancient tables provided the sun's mean longitude.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23">
        <a href="#cite_note-23">
         [16]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24">
        <a href="#cite_note-24">
         [17]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <a href="/wiki/Christopher_Clavius" title="Christopher Clavius">
        Christopher Clavius
       </a>
       , the architect of the Gregorian calendar, noted that the tables agreed neither on the time when the sun passed through the vernal equinox nor on the length of the mean tropical year. Tycho Brahe also noticed discrepancies.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25">
        <a href="#cite_note-25">
         [18]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26">
        <a href="#cite_note-26">
         [19]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The Gregorian leap year rule (97 leap years in 400 years) was put forward by Petrus Pitatus of Verona in 1560. He noted that it is consistent with the tropical year of the Alfonsine tables and with the mean tropical year of Copernicus (
       <i>
        De revolutionibus
       </i>
       ) and Reinhold (
       <i>
        Prutenic tables
       </i>
       ). The three mean tropical years in Babylonian sexagesimals as the excess over 365 days (the way they would have been extracted from the tables of mean longitude) were 14,33,9,57 (Alphonsine), 14,33,11,12 (Copernicus) and 14,33,9,24 (Reinhold). All values are the same to two places (14:33) and this is also the mean length of the Gregorian year. Thus Pitatus' solution would have commended itself to the astronomers.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27">
        <a href="#cite_note-27">
         [20]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Lilius's proposals had two components. Firstly, he proposed a correction to the length of the year. The mean
       <a href="/wiki/Tropical_year" title="Tropical year">
        tropical year
       </a>
       is 365.24219 days long.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28">
        <a href="#cite_note-28">
         [21]
        </a>
       </sup>
       As the average length of a Julian year is 365.25 days, the Julian year is almost 11 minutes longer than the mean tropical year. The discrepancy results in a drift of about three days every 400 years. Lilius's proposal resulted in an average year of 365.2425 days (see
       <a href="#Accuracy">
        Accuracy
       </a>
       ). At the time of Gregory's reform there had already been a drift of 10 days since the Council of Nicaea, resulting in the vernal equinox falling on 10 or 11 March instead of the ecclesiastically fixed date of 21 March, and if unreformed it would drift further. Lilius proposed that the 10-day drift should be corrected by deleting the Julian leap day on each of its ten occurrences over a period of forty years, thereby providing for a gradual return of the equinox to 21 March.
      </p>
      <p>
       Lilius's work was expanded upon by
       <a href="/wiki/Christopher_Clavius" title="Christopher Clavius">
        Christopher Clavius
       </a>
       in a closely argued, 800-page volume. He would later defend his and Lilius's work against detractors. Clavius's opinion was that the correction should take place in one move, and it was this advice which prevailed with Gregory.
      </p>
      <p>
       The second component consisted of an approximation which would provide an accurate yet simple, rule-based calendar. Lilius's formula was a 10-day correction to revert the drift since the Council of Nicaea, and the imposition of a leap day in only 97 years in 400 rather than in 1 year in 4. The proposed rule was that
       <i>
        years divisible by 100 would be leap years only if they were divisible by 400 as well
       </i>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       The 19-year cycle used for the lunar calendar was also to be corrected by one day every 300 or 400 years (8 times in 2500 years) along with corrections for the years that are no longer leap years (i.e., 1700, 1800, 1900, 2100, etc.). In fact, a new method for computing the date of Easter was introduced.
      </p>
      <p>
       When the new calendar was put in use, the error accumulated in the 13 centuries since the Council of Nicaea was corrected by a deletion of 10 days. The Julian calendar day Thursday, 4 October 1582 was followed by the first day of the Gregorian calendar, Friday, 15 October 1582 (the cycle of weekdays was not affected).
      </p>
      <div style="clear:both;">
      </div>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Adoption">
        Adoption
        <span id="Timeline">
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Adoption_of_the_Gregorian_calendar" title="Adoption of the Gregorian calendar">
        Adoption of the Gregorian calendar
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       Although Gregory's reform was enacted in the most solemn of forms available to the Church, the bull had no authority beyond the Catholic Church and the
       <a href="/wiki/Papal_States" title="Papal States">
        Papal States
       </a>
       . The changes that he was proposing were changes to the civil calendar, over which he had no authority. They required adoption by the civil authorities in each country to have legal effect.
      </p>
      <p>
       The bull
       <i>
        Inter gravissimas
       </i>
       became the law of the Catholic Church in 1582, but it was not recognised by
       <a href="/wiki/Protestantism" title="Protestantism">
        Protestant Churches
       </a>
       ,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Orthodox_Churches" title="Orthodox Churches">
        Orthodox Churches
       </a>
       , and a few others. Consequently, the days on which Easter and related holidays were celebrated by different Christian Churches again diverged.
      </p>
      <p>
       A month after having decreed the reform, the pope with a brief of 3 April 1582 granted to Antonio Lilio, the brother of Luigi Lilio, the exclusive right to publish the calendar for a period of ten years. The
       <i>
        Lunario Novo secondo la nuova riforma
       </i>
       printed by Vincenzo Accolti, one of the first calendars printed in Rome after the reform, notes at the bottom that it was signed with papal authorization and by Lilio (
       <i>
        Con licentia delli Superiori... et permissu Ant(onii) Lilij
       </i>
       ). The papal brief was later revoked, on 20 September 1582, because Antonio Lilio proved unable to keep up with the demand for copies.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29">
        <a href="#cite_note-29">
         [22]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       On 29 September 1582,
       <a href="/wiki/Philip_II_of_Spain" title="Philip II of Spain">
        Philip II of Spain
       </a>
       decreed the change from the Julian to the Gregorian calendar.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30">
        <a href="#cite_note-30">
         [23]
        </a>
       </sup>
       This affected much of Roman Catholic Europe, as Philip was at the time ruler over
       <a href="/wiki/Iberian_Union" title="Iberian Union">
        Spain and Portugal
       </a>
       as well as
       <a href="/wiki/History_of_Italy_(1559%E2%80%931814)" title="History of Italy (1559–1814)">
        much of Italy
       </a>
       . In these territories, as well as in the
       <a href="/wiki/Polish%E2%80%93Lithuanian_Commonwealth" title="Polish–Lithuanian Commonwealth">
        Polish–Lithuanian Commonwealth
       </a>
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
          <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2016)">
           citation needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
       (ruled by
       <a href="/wiki/Anna_Jagiellon" title="Anna Jagiellon">
        Anna Jagiellon
       </a>
       ) and in the Papal States, the new calendar was implemented on the date specified by the bull, with Julian Thursday, 4 October 1582, being followed by Gregorian Friday, 15 October 1582. The
       <a href="/wiki/Spanish_Empire" title="Spanish Empire">
        Spanish
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Portuguese_Empire" title="Portuguese Empire">
        Portuguese colonies
       </a>
       followed somewhat later
       <i>
        de facto
       </i>
       because of delay in communication.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31">
        <a href="#cite_note-31">
         [24]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Many
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Protestant" title="Protestant">
        Protestant
       </a>
       countries initially objected to adopting a Catholic innovation; some Protestants feared the new calendar was part of a plot to return them to the Catholic fold. For example, the British could not bring themselves to adopt the Catholic system explicitly: the Annexe to their
       <a href="/wiki/Calendar_(New_Style)_Act_1750" title="Calendar (New Style) Act 1750">
        Calendar (New Style) Act 1750
       </a>
       established a computation for the date of Easter that achieved the same result as Gregory's rules, without actually referring to him.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32">
        <a href="#cite_note-32">
         [25]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Great_Britain" title="Kingdom of Great Britain">
        Britain
       </a>
       and the
       <a href="/wiki/British_Empire" title="British Empire">
        British Empire
       </a>
       (including the eastern part of what is now the United States) adopted the Gregorian calendar in 1752.
       <a href="/wiki/Swedish_calendar" title="Swedish calendar">
        Sweden
       </a>
       followed in 1753.
      </p>
      <p>
       Prior to 1917, Turkey used the lunar
       <a href="/wiki/Islamic_calendar" title="Islamic calendar">
        Islamic calendar
       </a>
       with the
       <a href="/wiki/Hijri_year" title="Hijri year">
        Hegira era
       </a>
       for general purposes and the Julian calendar for fiscal purposes. The start of the fiscal year was eventually fixed at 1 March and the year number was roughly equivalent to the Hegira year (see
       <a href="/wiki/Rumi_calendar" title="Rumi calendar">
        Rumi calendar
       </a>
       ). As the solar year is longer than the lunar year this originally entailed the use of "escape years" every so often when the number of the fiscal year would jump. From 1 March 1917 the fiscal year became Gregorian, rather than Julian. On 1 January 1926 the use of the Gregorian calendar was extended to include use for general purposes and the number of the year became the same as in most other countries.
      </p>
      <table class="wikitable">

      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Difference_between_Gregorian_and_Julian_calendar_dates">
        Difference between Gregorian and Julian calendar dates
       </span>
      </h2>
      <table class="wikitable" style="float: right">
       <caption>
        Conversion from Julian to Gregorian dates.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34">
         <a href="#cite_note-34">
          [26]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </caption>
       <tr>
        <th>
         Gregorian range
        </th>
        <th>
         Julian range
        </th>
        <th>
         Difference
        </th>
       </tr>
       <tr align="right">
        <td>
         From 15 October 1582
         <br/>
         to 28 February 1700
        </td>
        <td>
         From 5 October 1582
         <br/>
         to 18 February 1700
        </td>
        <td align="center">
         10 days
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr align="right">
        <td>
         From 1 March 1700
         <br/>
         to 28 February 1800
        </td>
        <td>
         From 19 February 1700
         <br/>
         to 17 February 1800
        </td>
        <td align="center">
         11 days
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr align="right">
        <td>
         From 1 March 1800
         <br/>
         to 28 February 1900
        </td>
        <td>
         From 18 February 1800
         <br/>
         to 16 February 1900
        </td>
        <td align="center">
         12 days
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr align="right">
        <td>
         From 1 March 1900
         <br/>
         to 28 February 2100
        </td>
        <td>
         From 17 February 1900
         <br/>
         to 15 February 2100
        </td>
        <td align="center">
         13 days
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr align="right">
        <td>
         From 1 March 2100
         <br/>
         to 28 February 2200
        </td>
        <td>
         From 16 February 2100
         <br/>
         to 14 February 2200
        </td>
        <td align="center">
         14 days
        </td>
       </tr>
      </table>
      <p>
       Since the introduction of the Gregorian calendar, the difference between Gregorian and Julian calendar dates has increased by three days every four centuries (all date ranges are inclusive):
      </p>
      <p>
       This section always places the intercalary day on
       <span class="nowrap">
        29 February
       </span>
       even though it was always obtained by doubling
       <span class="nowrap">
        24 February
       </span>
       (the
       <i>
        bissextum
       </i>
       (twice sixth) or bissextile day) until the late
       <a href="/wiki/Middle_Ages" title="Middle Ages">
        Middle Ages
       </a>
       . The Gregorian calendar is proleptic before 1582 (assumed to exist before 1582).
      </p>
      <p>
       The following equation gives the number of days (actually, dates) that the Gregorian calendar is ahead of the Julian calendar, called the
       <i>
        secular difference
       </i>
       between the two calendars. A negative difference means the Julian calendar is ahead of the Gregorian calendar.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35">
        <a href="#cite_note-35">
         [27]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <span class="mwe-math-element">
         <span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">
          <math alttext="{\displaystyle D=\left\lfloor {Y/100}\right\rfloor -\left\lfloor {Y/400}\right\rfloor -2}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
           <semantics>
            <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
             <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
              <mi>
               D
              </mi>
              <mo>
               =
              </mo>
              <mrow>
               <mo>
                ⌊
               </mo>
               <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                <mi>
                 Y
                </mi>
                <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                 <mo>
                  /
                 </mo>
                </mrow>
                <mn>
                 100
                </mn>
               </mrow>
               <mo>
                ⌋
               </mo>
              </mrow>
              <mo>
               −
               <!-- − -->
              </mo>
              <mrow>
               <mo>
                ⌊
               </mo>
               <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                <mi>
                 Y
                </mi>
                <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                 <mo>
                  /
                 </mo>
                </mrow>
                <mn>
                 400
                </mn>
               </mrow>
               <mo>
                ⌋
               </mo>
              </mrow>
              <mo>
               −
               <!-- − -->
              </mo>
              <mn>
               2
              </mn>
             </mstyle>
            </mrow>
            <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">
             {\displaystyle D=\left\lfloor {Y/100}\right\rfloor -\left\lfloor {Y/400}\right\rfloor -2}
            </annotation>
           </semantics>
          </math>
         </span>
         <img alt="D=\left\lfloor {Y/100}\right\rfloor -\left\lfloor {Y/400}\right\rfloor -2" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/42d427f32ab31da2c2c80dde14ea44c5321aff5d" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:28.842ex; height:2.843ex;"/>
        </span>
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <p>
       where
       <span class="mwe-math-element">
        <span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">
         <math alttext="{\displaystyle D}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
          <semantics>
           <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
            <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
             <mi>
              D
             </mi>
            </mstyle>
           </mrow>
           <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">
            {\displaystyle D}
           </annotation>
          </semantics>
         </math>
        </span>
        <img alt="D" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/f34a0c600395e5d4345287e21fb26efd386990e6" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:1.924ex; height:2.176ex;"/>
       </span>
       is the secular difference and
       <span class="mwe-math-element">
        <span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">
         <math alttext="{\displaystyle Y}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
          <semantics>
           <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
            <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
             <mi>
              Y
             </mi>
            </mstyle>
           </mrow>
           <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">
            {\displaystyle Y}
           </annotation>
          </semantics>
         </math>
        </span>
        <img alt="Y" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/961d67d6b454b4df2301ac571808a3538b3a6d3f" style="vertical-align: -0.171ex; width:1.773ex; height:2.009ex;"/>
       </span>
       is the year using
       <a href="/wiki/Astronomical_year_numbering" title="Astronomical year numbering">
        astronomical year numbering
       </a>
       , that is, use
       <span class="nowrap">
        (year BC) − 1
       </span>
       for BC years.
       <span class="mwe-math-element">
        <span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">
         <math alttext="{\displaystyle \left\lfloor {x}\right\rfloor }" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
          <semantics>
           <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
            <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
             <mrow>
              <mo>
               ⌊
              </mo>
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
               <mi>
                x
               </mi>
              </mrow>
              <mo>
               ⌋
              </mo>
             </mrow>
            </mstyle>
           </mrow>
           <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">
            {\displaystyle \left\lfloor {x}\right\rfloor }
           </annotation>
          </semantics>
         </math>
        </span>
        <img alt="\left\lfloor {x}\right\rfloor " aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/646b67c5ebaa8a43d4687159565e622d08d25baf" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:3.394ex; height:2.843ex;"/>
       </span>
       means that if the result of the division is not an integer it is rounded down to the nearest integer. Thus during the 1900s, 1900/400 = 4, while during the −500s, −500/400 = −2.
      </p>
      <p>
       The general rule, in years which are leap years in the Julian calendar but not the Gregorian, is as follows:
      </p>
      <p>
       Up to 28 February in the calendar you are converting
       <em>
        from
       </em>
       add one day less or subtract one day more than the calculated value. Remember to give February the appropriate number of days for the calendar you are converting
       <em>
        into
       </em>
       . When you are subtracting days to move from Julian to Gregorian be careful, when calculating the Gregorian equivalent of 29 February (Julian), to remember that 29 February is discounted. Thus if the calculated value is −4 the Gregorian equivalent of this date is 24 February.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36">
        <a href="#cite_note-36">
         [28]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37">
        <a href="#cite_note-37">
         [29]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <div style="clear:both;">
      </div>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Beginning_of_the_year">
        Beginning of the year
       </span>
      </h2>
      <table class="sortable wikitable" style="float: right">
