      <p>
       The year used in dates during the
       <a href="/wiki/Roman_Republic" title="Roman Republic">
        Roman Republic
       </a>
       and the
       <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire" title="Roman Empire">
        Roman Empire
       </a>
       was the consular year, which began on the day when consuls first entered office—probably 1 May before 222 BC, 15 March from 222 BC and 1 January from 153 BC.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45">
        <a href="#cite_note-45">
         [36]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The Julian calendar, which began in 45 BC, continued to use 1 January as the first day of the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/New_year" title="New year">
        new year
       </a>
       . Even though the year used for dates changed, the civil year always displayed its months in the order January to December from the Roman Republican period until the present.
      </p>
      <p>
       During the Middle Ages, under the influence of the Catholic Church, many Western European countries moved the start of the year to one of several important Christian festivals—25 December (supposed
       <a href="/wiki/Nativity_of_Jesus" title="Nativity of Jesus">
        Nativity of Jesus
       </a>
       ), 25 March (
       <a href="/wiki/Annunciation" title="Annunciation">
        Annunciation
       </a>
       ), or Easter (France),
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MS_46-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-MS-46">
         [37]
        </a>
       </sup>
       while the Byzantine Empire began its year on 1 September and Russia did so on 1 March until 1492 when the new year was moved to 1 September.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47">
        <a href="#cite_note-47">
         [38]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       In common usage, 1 January was regarded as New Year's Day and celebrated as such,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48">
        <a href="#cite_note-48">
         [39]
        </a>
       </sup>
       but from the 12th century until 1751 the legal year in England began on 25 March (
       <a href="/wiki/Lady_Day" title="Lady Day">
        Lady Day
       </a>
       ).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49">
        <a href="#cite_note-49">
         [40]
        </a>
       </sup>
       So, for example, the Parliamentary record lists the execution of
       <a href="/wiki/Charles_I_of_England" title="Charles I of England">
        Charles I
       </a>
       on 30 January as occurring in 1648 (as the year did not end until 24 March),
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50">
        <a href="#cite_note-50">
         [41]
        </a>
       </sup>
       although later histories adjust the start of the year to 1 January and record the execution as occurring in 1649.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EC-NA_51-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-EC-NA-51">
         [42]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Most Western European countries changed the start of the year to 1 January before they adopted the Gregorian calendar. For example, Scotland changed the start of the Scottish New Year to 1 January in 1600 (this means that 1599 was a short year). England, Ireland and the British colonies changed the start of the year to 1 January in 1752 (so 1751 was a short year with only 282 days) though in England the start of the tax year remained at 25 March (O.S.), 5 April (N.S.) till 1800, when it moved to 6 April. Later in 1752 in September the Gregorian calendar was introduced throughout Britain and the British colonies (see the section
       <a href="#Adoption">
        Adoption
       </a>
       ). These two reforms were implemented by the
       <a href="/wiki/Calendar_(New_Style)_Act_1750" title="Calendar (New Style) Act 1750">
        Calendar (New Style) Act 1750
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NT-Perpetual-Calendar_52-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-NT-Perpetual-Calendar-52">
         [43]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       In some countries, an official decree or law specified that the start of the year should be 1 January. For such countries a specific year when a 1 January-year became the norm can be identified. In other countries the customs varied, and the start of the year moved back and forth as fashion and influence from other countries dictated various customs.
      </p>
      <p>
       Neither the papal bull nor its attached canons explicitly fix such a date, though it is implied by two tables of
       <a href="/wiki/Saint" title="Saint">
        saint
       </a>
       's days, one labelled 1582 which ends on 31 December, and another for any full year that begins on 1 January. It also specifies its epact relative to 1 January, in contrast with the Julian calendar, which specified it relative to 22 March. The old date was derived from the Greek system: the earlier
       <i>
        Supputatio Romana
       </i>
       specified it relative to 1 January.
      </p>
      <div class="reflist" style="list-style-type: decimal;">
       <div class="mw-references-wrap">
        <ol class="references">
         <li id="cite_note-40">
          <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
           <b>
            <a href="#cite_ref-40">
             ^
            </a>
           </b>
          </span>
          <span class="reference-text">
           In 1793 France abandoned the Gregorian calendar in favour of the
           <a href="/wiki/French_Republican_Calendar" title="French Republican Calendar">
            French Republican Calendar
           </a>
           . This change was reverted in 1805.
          </span>
         </li>
        </ol>
       </div>
      </div>
      <div style="clear:both;">
      </div>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Dual_dating">
        Dual dating
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Dual_dating" title="Dual dating">
        Dual dating
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       During the period between 1582, when the first countries adopted the Gregorian calendar, and 1923, when the last European country adopted it, it was often necessary to indicate the date of some event in both the Julian calendar and in the Gregorian calendar, for example, "10/21 February 1750/51", where the dual year accounts for some countries already beginning their numbered year on 1 January while others were still using some other date. Even before 1582, the year sometimes had to be double dated because of the different beginnings of the year in various countries. Woolley, writing in his biography of
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/John_Dee_(mathematician)" title="John Dee (mathematician)">
        John Dee
       </a>
       (1527–1608/9), notes that immediately after 1582 English letter writers "customarily" used "two dates" on their letters, one OS and one NS.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53">
        <a href="#cite_note-53">
         [44]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Old_Style_and_New_Style_dates">
        Old Style and New Style dates
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main articles:
       <a href="/wiki/Old_Style_and_New_Style_dates" title="Old Style and New Style dates">
        Old Style and New Style dates
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Dual_dating" title="Dual dating">
        Dual dating
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       "Old Style" (OS) and "New Style" (NS) are sometimes added to dates to identify which calendar reference system is used for the date given. In Britain and its Colonies, where the
       <a href="/wiki/Calendar_(New_Style)_Act_1750" title="Calendar (New Style) Act 1750">
        Calendar Act of 1750
       </a>
       altered the start of the year,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54">
        <a href="#cite_note-54">
         [Note 9]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and also aligned the British calendar with the Gregorian calendar, there is some confusion as to what these terms mean. They can indicate that the start of the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Julian_year_(calendar)" title="Julian year (calendar)">
        Julian year
       </a>
       has been adjusted to start on 1 January (NS) even though contemporary documents use a different start of year (OS); or to indicate that a date conforms to the Julian calendar (OS), formerly in use in many countries, rather than the Gregorian calendar (NS).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EC-NA_51-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-EC-NA-51">
         [42]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55">
        <a href="#cite_note-55">
         [45]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56">
        <a href="#cite_note-56">
         [46]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57">
        <a href="#cite_note-57">
         [47]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Proleptic_Gregorian_calendar">
        Proleptic Gregorian calendar
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Proleptic_Gregorian_calendar" title="Proleptic Gregorian calendar">
        Proleptic Gregorian calendar
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       Extending the Gregorian calendar backwards to dates preceding its official introduction produces
       <a href="/wiki/Proleptic_Gregorian_calendar" title="Proleptic Gregorian calendar">
        a proleptic calendar
       </a>
       , which should be used with some caution. For ordinary purposes, the dates of events occurring prior to 15 October 1582 are generally shown as they appeared in the Julian calendar, with the year starting on 1 January, and no conversion to their Gregorian equivalents. For example, the
       <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Agincourt" title="Battle of Agincourt">
        Battle of Agincourt
       </a>
       is universally considered to have been fought on 25 October 1415 which is
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Crispin" title="Crispin">
        Saint Crispin
       </a>
       's Day.
      </p>
      <p>
       Usually, the mapping of new dates onto old dates with a start of year adjustment works well with little confusion for events that happened before the introduction of the Gregorian calendar. But for the period between the first introduction of the Gregorian calendar on 15 October 1582 and its introduction in Britain on 14 September 1752, there can be considerable confusion between events in continental western Europe and in British domains in English language histories.
      </p>
      <p>
       Events in continental western Europe are usually reported in English language histories as happening under the Gregorian calendar. For example, the
       <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Blenheim" title="Battle of Blenheim">
        Battle of Blenheim
       </a>
       is always given as 13 August 1704. Confusion occurs when an event affects both. For example,
       <a href="/wiki/William_III_of_England" title="William III of England">
        William III of England
       </a>
       arrived at
       <a href="/wiki/Brixham" title="Brixham">
        Brixham
       </a>
       in England on 5 November 1688 (Julian calendar), after setting sail from the Netherlands on 11 November 1688 (Gregorian calendar).
      </p>
      <p>
       <a href="/wiki/William_Shakespeare" title="William Shakespeare">
        Shakespeare
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Miguel_de_Cervantes" title="Miguel de Cervantes">
        Cervantes
       </a>
       seemingly died on exactly the same date (23 April 1616), but Cervantes predeceased Shakespeare by ten days in real time (as Spain used the Gregorian calendar, but Britain used the Julian calendar). This coincidence encouraged
       <a href="/wiki/UNESCO" title="UNESCO">
        UNESCO
       </a>
       to make 23 April the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/World_Book_and_Copyright_Day" title="World Book and Copyright Day">
        World Book and Copyright Day
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       Astronomers avoid this ambiguity by the use of the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Julian_day_number" title="Julian day number">
        Julian day number
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       For dates before the year 1, unlike the proleptic Gregorian calendar used in the
       <a href="/wiki/International_standard" title="International standard">
        international standard
       </a>
       <a href="/wiki/ISO_8601" title="ISO 8601">
        ISO 8601
       </a>
       , the traditional proleptic Gregorian calendar (like the Julian calendar) does not have a
       <a href="/wiki/Year_zero" title="Year zero">
        year 0
       </a>
       and instead uses the ordinal numbers 1, 2, … both for years AD and BC. Thus the traditional time line is 2 BC, 1 BC, AD 1, and AD 2. ISO 8601 uses astronomical year numbering which includes a year 0 and negative numbers before it. Thus the ISO 8601 time line is
       <span class="nowrap">
        −0001
       </span>
       , 0000, 0001, and 0002.
      </p>
      <p>
       <span id="Month">
       </span>
       <span id="Months_of_the_year">
       </span>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Months">
        Months
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       The Gregorian calendar continued to employ the
       <a href="/wiki/Julian_calendar" title="Julian calendar">
        Julian
       </a>
       months, which have Latinate names and irregular numbers of
       <a href="/wiki/Day" title="Day">
        days
       </a>
       :
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/January" title="January">
         January
        </a>
        (31 days), from Latin
        <i>
         <span lang="la" xml:lang="la">
          mēnsis Iānuārius
         </span>
        </i>
        , "Month of
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Janus_(mythology)" title="Janus (mythology)">
         Janus
        </a>
        ",
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-janoed_58-0">
         <a href="#cite_note-janoed-58">
          [48]
         </a>
        </sup>
        the
        <a href="/wiki/Roman_mythology" title="Roman mythology">
         Roman god
        </a>
        of gates, doorways, beginnings and endings
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/February" title="February">
         February
        </a>
        (28 days in
        <a href="/wiki/Common_year" title="Common year">
         common
        </a>
        and 29 in
        <a href="/wiki/Leap_year" title="Leap year">
         leap years
        </a>
        ), from Latin
        <i>
         <span lang="la" xml:lang="la">
          mēnsis Februārius
         </span>
        </i>
        , "Month of the
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Februa" title="Februa">
         Februa
        </a>
        ", the
        <a href="/wiki/Roman_festivals" title="Roman festivals">
         Roman festival
        </a>
        of purgation and purification,
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-foed_59-0">
         <a href="#cite_note-foed-59">
          [49]
         </a>
        </sup>
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-oe_60-0">
         <a href="#cite_note-oe-60">
          [50]
         </a>
        </sup>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cognate_word" title="Cognate word">
         cognate
        </a>
        with
        <a href="/wiki/Fever" title="Fever">
         fever
        </a>
        ,
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-foed_59-1">
         <a href="#cite_note-foed-59">
          [49]
         </a>
        </sup>
        the
        <a href="/wiki/Etruscan_mythology" title="Etruscan mythology">
         Etruscan
        </a>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Death_god" title="Death god">
         death god
        </a>
        <a href="/wiki/Februus" title="Februus">
         Februus
        </a>
        ("Purifier"),
        <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
         [
         <i>
          <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
           <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2017)">
            citation needed
           </span>
          </a>
         </i>
         ]
        </sup>
        and the
        <a href="/wiki/Proto-Indo-European_language" title="Proto-Indo-European language">
         PIE
        </a>
        word for
        <a href="/wiki/Sulfur" title="Sulfur">
         sulfur
        </a>
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-foed_59-2">
         <a href="#cite_note-foed-59">
          [49]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/March_(month)" title="March (month)">
         March
        </a>
        (31 days), from Latin
        <i>
         <span lang="la" xml:lang="la">
          mēnsis Mārtius
         </span>
        </i>
        , "Month of
        <a href="/wiki/Mars_(mythology)" title="Mars (mythology)">
         Mars
        </a>
        ",
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-maroed_61-0">
         <a href="#cite_note-maroed-61">
          [51]
         </a>
        </sup>
        the Roman
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/War_god" title="War god">
         war god
        </a>
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-oe_60-1">
         <a href="#cite_note-oe-60">
          [50]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/April_(month)" title="April (month)">
         April
        </a>
        (30 days), from Latin
        <i>
         <span lang="la" xml:lang="la">
          mēnsis Aprīlis
         </span>
        </i>
        , of uncertain meaning
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-aproed_62-0">
         <a href="#cite_note-aproed-62">
          [52]
         </a>
        </sup>
        but usually derived from some form of the verb
        <i>
         <span lang="la" xml:lang="la">
          aperire
         </span>
        </i>
        ("to open")
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63">
         <a href="#cite_note-63">
          [53]
         </a>
        </sup>
        or the name of the goddess
        <a href="/wiki/Aphrodite" title="Aphrodite">
         Aphrodite
        </a>
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-oe_60-3">
         <a href="#cite_note-oe-60">
          [50]
         </a>
        </sup>
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67">
         <a href="#cite_note-67">
          [57]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/May_(month)" title="May (month)">
         May
        </a>
        (31 days), from Latin
        <i>
         <span lang="la" xml:lang="la">
          mēnsis Māius
         </span>
        </i>
        , "Month of
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Maia_Maiestas" title="Maia Maiestas">
         Maia
        </a>
        ",
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mayoed_68-0">
         <a href="#cite_note-mayoed-68">
          [58]
         </a>
        </sup>
        a Roman
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vegetation_goddess" title="Vegetation goddess">
         vegetation goddess
        </a>
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-oe_60-4">
         <a href="#cite_note-oe-60">
          [50]
         </a>
        </sup>
        whose name is cognate with Latin
        <i>
         <span lang="la" xml:lang="la">
          magnus
         </span>
        </i>
        ("great")
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mayoed_68-1">
         <a href="#cite_note-mayoed-68">
          [58]
         </a>
        </sup>
        and English
        <i>
         major
        </i>
       </li>
       <li>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/June_(month)" title="June (month)">
         June
        </a>
        (30 days), from Latin
        <i>
         <span lang="la" xml:lang="la">
          mēnsis Iūnius
         </span>
        </i>
        , "Month of
        <a href="/wiki/Juno_(mythology)" title="Juno (mythology)">
         Juno
        </a>
        ",
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-junoed_69-0">
         <a href="#cite_note-junoed-69">
          [59]
         </a>
        </sup>
        the Roman goddess of
        <a href="/wiki/Marriage_in_ancient_Rome" title="Marriage in ancient Rome">
         marriage
        </a>
        ,
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Childbirth_goddess" title="Childbirth goddess">
         childbirth
        </a>
        , and rule
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-oe_60-5">
         <a href="#cite_note-oe-60">
          [50]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/July" title="July">
         July
        </a>
        (31 days), from Latin
        <i>
         <span lang="la" xml:lang="la">
          mēnsis Iūlius
         </span>
        </i>
        , "Month of
        <a href="/wiki/Julius_Caesar" title="Julius Caesar">
         Julius Caesar
        </a>
        ", the month of Caesar's birth, instituted in 44
        <span class="nowrap">
        </span>
        <span class="smallcaps">
         <span style="font-variant: small-caps; text-transform: lowercase;">
          BC
         </span>
        </span>
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-juloed_70-0">
         <a href="#cite_note-juloed-70">
          [60]
         </a>
        </sup>
        as part of
        <a href="/wiki/Julian_calendar" title="Julian calendar">
         his calendrical reforms
        </a>
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-oe_60-6">
         <a href="#cite_note-oe-60">
          [50]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/August_(month)" title="August (month)">
         August
        </a>
        (31 days), from Latin
        <i>
         <span lang="la" xml:lang="la">
          mēnsis Augustus
         </span>
        </i>
        , "Month of
        <a href="/wiki/Augustus" title="Augustus">
         Augustus
        </a>
        ", instituted by Augustus in 8
        <span class="nowrap">
        </span>
        <span class="smallcaps">
         <span style="font-variant: small-caps; text-transform: lowercase;">
          BC
         </span>
        </span>
        in agreement with July and from the occurrence during the month of several important events during his rise to power
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-augoed_71-0">
         <a href="#cite_note-augoed-71">
          [61]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/September" title="September">
         September
        </a>
        (30 days), from Latin
        <i>
         <span lang="la" xml:lang="la">
          mēnsis september
         </span>
        </i>
        , "seventh month", from its position in the
        <a href="/wiki/Roman_calendar" title="Roman calendar">
         Roman calendar
        </a>
        before 153
        <span class="nowrap">
        </span>
        <span class="smallcaps">
         <span style="font-variant: small-caps; text-transform: lowercase;">
          BC
         </span>
        </span>
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-septoed_72-0">
         <a href="#cite_note-septoed-72">
          [62]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/October" title="October">
         October
        </a>
        (31 days), from Latin
        <i>
         <span lang="la" xml:lang="la">
          mēnsis octōber
         </span>
        </i>
        , "eighth month",
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ooed_73-0">
         <a href="#cite_note-ooed-73">
          [63]
         </a>
        </sup>
        from its position in the Roman calendar before 153
        <span class="nowrap">
        </span>
        <span class="smallcaps">
         <span style="font-variant: small-caps; text-transform: lowercase;">
          BC
         </span>
        </span>
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-septoed_72-1">
         <a href="#cite_note-septoed-72">
          [62]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/November" title="November">
         November
        </a>
        (30 days), from Latin
        <i>
         <span lang="la" xml:lang="la">
          mēnsis november
         </span>
        </i>
        , "ninth month",
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-noed_74-0">
         <a href="#cite_note-noed-74">
          [64]
         </a>
        </sup>
        from its position in the Roman calendar before 153
        <span class="nowrap">
        </span>
        <span class="smallcaps">
         <span style="font-variant: small-caps; text-transform: lowercase;">
          BC
         </span>
        </span>
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-septoed_72-2">
         <a href="#cite_note-septoed-72">
          [62]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/December" title="December">
         December
        </a>
        (31 days), from Latin
        <i>
         <span lang="la" xml:lang="la">
          mēnsis december
         </span>
        </i>
        , "tenth month",
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-doed_75-0">
         <a href="#cite_note-doed-75">
          [65]
         </a>
        </sup>
        from its position in the Roman calendar before 153
        <span class="nowrap">
        </span>
        <span class="smallcaps">
         <span style="font-variant: small-caps; text-transform: lowercase;">
          BC
         </span>
        </span>
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-septoed_72-3">
         <a href="#cite_note-septoed-72">
          [62]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
      </ul>
      <p>
       Europeans sometimes attempt to remember the number of days in each month by memorizing some form of the traditional verse "
       <a href="/wiki/Thirty_Days_Hath_September" title="Thirty Days Hath September">
        Thirty Days Hath September
       </a>
       ". It appears in Latin,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lucy_76-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-lucy-76">
         [66]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-annie_77-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-annie-77">
         [67]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Italian,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-i4d_78-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-i4d-78">
         [68]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and French,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79">
        <a href="#cite_note-79">
         [69]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and belongs to a broad
       <a href="/wiki/Oral_tradition" title="Oral tradition">
        oral tradition
       </a>
       but the earliest currently attested form of the poem is the English marginalia inserted into a
       <a href="/wiki/Calendar_of_saints" title="Calendar of saints">
        calendar of saints
       </a>
       <abbr title="circa">
        c.
       </abbr>
       <span style="white-space:nowrap;">
        1425
       </span>
       :
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wales_80-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-wales-80">
         [70]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tto_81-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-tto-81">
         [71]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82">
        <a href="#cite_note-82">
         [72]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <table>
       <tr>
        <td>
         <blockquote>
          <p>
           <span class="nowrap">
            Thirti dayes hath novembir
           </span>
           <br/>
           <span class="nowrap">
            April june and Septembir.
           </span>
           <br/>
           <span class="nowrap">
            Of xxviij is but oon
           </span>
           <br/>
           <span class="nowrap">
            And alle the remenaunt xxx and j
            <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tto_81-1">
             <a href="#cite_note-tto-81">
              [71]
             </a>
            </sup>
           </span>
          </p>
         </blockquote>
        </td>
        <td>
         <span style="white-space:nowrap">
          <span class="nowrap">
          </span>
          <span style="margin-left:8em">
           <span class="nowrap">
           </span>
          </span>
         </span>
        </td>
        <td>
         <blockquote>
          <p>
           <span class="nowrap">
            Thirty days have November,
           </span>
           <br/>
           <span class="nowrap">
            April, June, and September.
           </span>
           <br/>
           <span class="nowrap">
            Of 28 is but one
           </span>
           <br/>
           <span class="nowrap">
            And all the remnant 30 and 1.
           </span>
          </p>
         </blockquote>
        </td>
       </tr>
      </table>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Month_-_Knuckles_(en).svg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="220" data-file-width="450" height="108" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Month_-_Knuckles_%28en%29.svg/220px-Month_-_Knuckles_%28en%29.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Month_-_Knuckles_%28en%29.svg/330px-Month_-_Knuckles_%28en%29.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Month_-_Knuckles_%28en%29.svg/440px-Month_-_Knuckles_%28en%29.svg.png 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Month_-_Knuckles_(en).svg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         The
         <a href="/wiki/Knuckle_mnemonic" title="Knuckle mnemonic">
          knuckle mnemonic
         </a>
         for the days of the months of the year
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       Variations appeared in
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Mother_Goose" title="Mother Goose">
         Mother Goose
        </a>
       </i>
       and continue to be taught at schools. The unhelpfulness of such involved
       <a href="/wiki/Mnemonic" title="Mnemonic">
        mnemonics
       </a>
       has been parodied as "Thirty days hath September
       <span class="nowrap">
       </span>
       / But all the rest I can't remember"
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83">
        <a href="#cite_note-83">
         [73]
        </a>
       </sup>
       but it has also been called "probably the only sixteenth-century poem most ordinary citizens know by heart".
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-holly_84-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-holly-84">
         [74]
        </a>
       </sup>
       A common nonverbal alternative is the
       <a href="/wiki/Knuckle_mnemonic" title="Knuckle mnemonic">
        knuckle mnemonic
       </a>
       , considering the
       <a href="/wiki/Knuckle" title="Knuckle">
        knuckles
       </a>
       of one's
       <a href="/wiki/Hand" title="Hand">
        hands
       </a>
       as months with 31 days and the lower spaces between them as the months with fewer days. Using two hands, one may start from either
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pinkie_finger" title="Pinkie finger">
        pinkie
       </a>
       knuckle as January and count across, omitting the space between the
       <a href="/wiki/Index_finger" title="Index finger">
        index
       </a>
       knuckles (July and August). The same procedure can be done using the knuckles of a single hand, returning from the last (July) to the first (August) and continuing through. A similar mnemonic is to move up a
       <a href="/wiki/Musical_keyboard" title="Musical keyboard">
        piano keyboard
       </a>
       in
       <a href="/wiki/Semitone" title="Semitone">
        semitones
       </a>
       from an F key, taking the white keys as the longer months and the black keys as the shorter ones.
      </p>
      <p>
       <span id="Week">
       </span>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Weeks">
        Weeks
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Seven-day_week" title="Seven-day week">
        Seven-day week
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       In conjunction with the system of months there is a system of
       <a href="/wiki/Week" title="Week">
        weeks
       </a>
       . A physical or electronic calendar provides conversion from a given date to the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Weekday" title="Weekday">
        weekday
       </a>
       , and shows multiple dates for a given weekday and month.
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Calculating_the_day_of_the_week" title="Calculating the day of the week">
        Calculating the day of the week
       </a>
       is not very simple, because of the irregularities in the Gregorian system. When the Gregorian calendar was adopted by each country, the weekly cycle continued uninterrupted. For example, in the case of the few countries that adopted the reformed calendar on the date proposed by Gregory XIII for the calendar's adoption, Friday, 15 October 1582, the preceding date was Thursday, 4 October 1582 (Julian calendar).
      </p>
      <p>
       Opinions vary about the numbering of the days of the week. ISO 8601, in common use worldwide, starts with Monday=1; printed monthly calendar grids often list Mondays in the first (left) column of dates and Sundays in the last. Software often starts with Sunday=0, which places Sundays in the left column of a monthly calendar page.
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Accuracy">
        Accuracy
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       The Gregorian calendar improves the approximation made by the Julian calendar by skipping three Julian leap days in every 400 years, giving an average year of 365.2425
       <a href="/wiki/Solar_time" title="Solar time">
        mean solar days
       </a>
       long.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85">
        <a href="#cite_note-85">
         [75]
        </a>
       </sup>
       This approximation has an error of about one day per 3,030 years
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86">
        <a href="#cite_note-86">
         [76]
        </a>
       </sup>
       with respect to the current value of the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mean_tropical_year" title="Mean tropical year">
        mean tropical year
       </a>
       . However, because of the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Axial_precession_(astronomy)" title="Axial precession (astronomy)">
        precession of the equinoxes
       </a>
       , which is not constant, and the movement of the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Perihelion" title="Perihelion">
        perihelion
       </a>
       (which affects the Earth's orbital speed) the error with respect to the
       <i>
        astronomical
       </i>
       vernal equinox is variable; using the average interval between vernal equinoxes near 2000 of 365.24237 days
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87">
        <a href="#cite_note-87">
         [77]
        </a>
       </sup>
       implies an error closer to 1 day every 7,700 years. By any criterion, the Gregorian calendar is substantially more accurate than the 1 day in 128 years error of the Julian calendar (average year 365.25 days).
      </p>
      <p>
       In the 19th century, Sir
       <a href="/wiki/John_Herschel" title="John Herschel">
        John Herschel
       </a>
       proposed a modification to the Gregorian calendar with 969 leap days every 4000 years, instead of 970 leap days that the Gregorian calendar would insert over the same period.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88">
        <a href="#cite_note-88">
         [78]
        </a>
       </sup>
       This would reduce the average year to 365.24225 days. Herschel's proposal would make the year 4000, and multiples thereof, common instead of leap. While this modification has often been proposed since, it has never been officially adopted.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89">
        <a href="#cite_note-89">
         [79]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       On time scales of thousands of years, the Gregorian calendar falls behind the astronomical seasons because the slowing down of the
       <a href="/wiki/Earth%27s_rotation" title="Earth's rotation">
        Earth's rotation
       </a>
       makes each day slightly longer over time (see
       <a href="/wiki/Tidal_acceleration" title="Tidal acceleration">
        tidal acceleration
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Leap_second" title="Leap second">
        leap second
       </a>
       ) while the year maintains a more uniform duration.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Calendar_seasonal_error">
        Calendar seasonal error
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Gregoriancalendarleap_solstice.svg" title="Gregorian calendar seasons difference">
        <img alt="Gregorian calendar seasons difference" data-file-height="420" data-file-width="800" height="420" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/Gregoriancalendarleap_solstice.svg/800px-Gregoriancalendarleap_solstice.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/Gregoriancalendarleap_solstice.svg/1200px-Gregoriancalendarleap_solstice.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/Gregoriancalendarleap_solstice.svg/1600px-Gregoriancalendarleap_solstice.svg.png 2x" width="800"/>
       </a>
      </p>
      <p>
       This image shows the difference between the Gregorian calendar and the astronomical seasons.
      </p>
      <p>
       The
       <i>
        y
       </i>
       -axis is the date in June and the
       <i>
        x
       </i>
       -axis is Gregorian calendar years.
      </p>
      <p>
       Each point is the date and time of the
       <a href="/wiki/Solstice" title="Solstice">
        June solstice
       </a>
       in that particular year. The error shifts by about a quarter of a day per year. Centurial years are ordinary years, unless they are divisible by 400, in which case they are leap years. This causes a correction in the years 1700, 1800, 1900, 2100, 2200, and 2300.
      </p>
      <p>
       For instance, these corrections cause 23 December 1903 to be the latest December solstice, and 20 December 2096 to be the earliest solstice—about 2.35 days of variation compared with the seasonal event.
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Proposed_reforms">
        Proposed reforms
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       The following are
       <a href="/wiki/Calendar_reform" title="Calendar reform">
        proposed reforms
       </a>
       of the Gregorian calendar:
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>

       </li>
       <li>

         International Fixed Calendar
        </a>
        (also called the
        <i>
         International Perpetual calendar
        </i>
        )
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/World_Calendar" title="World Calendar">
         World Calendar
        </a>
       </li>
       <li>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/World_Season_Calendar" title="World Season Calendar">
         World Season Calendar
        </a>
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Leap_week_calendar" title="Leap week calendar">
         Leap week calendars
        </a>
        <ul>
         <li>
          <a href="/wiki/Pax_Calendar" title="Pax Calendar">
           Pax Calendar
          </a>
         </li>
         <li>
          <a href="/wiki/Symmetry454" title="Symmetry454">
           Symmetry454
          </a>
         </li>
         <li>
          <a href="/wiki/Hanke%E2%80%93Henry_Permanent_Calendar" title="Hanke–Henry Permanent Calendar">

          </a>
         </li>
        </ul>
       </li>
      </ul>

      <p>
       <b>
        Precursors of the Gregorian reform
       </b>
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Johannes_de_Sacrobosco" title="Johannes de Sacrobosco">
         Johannes de Sacrobosco
        </a>
        ,
        <i>
         De Anni Ratione
        </i>
        ("On reckoning the years"), c. 1235
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Roger_Bacon" title="Roger Bacon">
         Roger Bacon
        </a>
        ,
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Opus_Majus" title="Opus Majus">
          Opus Majus
         </a>
        </i>
        ("Greater Work"), c. 1267
       </li>

      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">
        Notes
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="reflist" style="list-style-type: decimal;">
       <div class="mw-references-wrap">
        <ol class="references">
         <li id="cite_note-3">
          <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
           <b>
            <a href="#cite_ref-3">
             ^
            </a>
           </b>
          </span>
          <span class="reference-text">
           The international standard for the representation of dates and times,
           <a href="/wiki/ISO_8601" title="ISO 8601">
            ISO 8601
           </a>
           , uses the Gregorian calendar. Section 3.2.1.
          </span>
         </li>
         <li id="cite_note-8">
          <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
           <b>
            <a href="#cite_ref-8">
             ^
            </a>
           </b>
          </span>
          <span class="reference-text">
           In Great Britain and its colonies, "Old Style" typically implies that the date is given in the Julian calendar with the year beginning on 25 March, whereas "New Style" implies use of the Gregorian calendar with years beginning 1 January.
          </span>
         </li>
         <li id="cite_note-11">
          <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
           <b>
            <a href="#cite_ref-11">
             ^
            </a>
           </b>
          </span>
          <span class="reference-text">
           The first known occurrence of
           <i>
            Common Era
           </i>
           in English dates to 1708. Years before the beginning of the era are abbreviated in English as either BC for "Before Christ", or as BCE for "Before the Common Era". Two era names occur within the bull
           <i>
            <a href="/wiki/Inter_gravissimas" title="Inter gravissimas">
             Inter gravissimas
            </a>
           </i>
           itself,
           <i>
            <span lang="la" xml:lang="la">
             <i>
              anno Incarnationis dominicæ
             </i>
            </span>
           </i>
           ("in the year of the Incarnation of the Lord") for the year it was signed, and
           <i>
            <span lang="la" xml:lang="la">
             <i>
              anno à Nativitate Domini nostri Jesu Christi
             </i>
            </span>
           </i>
           ("in the year from the Nativity of our Lord
           <a href="/wiki/Jesus" title="Jesus">
            Jesus
           </a>
           Christ") for the year it was printed.
           <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10">
            <a href="#cite_note-10">
             [8]
            </a>
           </sup>
          </span>
         </li>
         <li id="cite_note-14">
          <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
           <b>
            <a href="#cite_ref-14">
             ^
            </a>
           </b>
          </span>
          <span class="reference-text">
           The cycle described applies to the solar, or civil, calendar. If one also considers the ecclesiastical lunar rules, the lunisolar Easter
           <i>
            computus
           </i>
           cycle repeats only after 5,700,000 years of 2,081,882,250 days in 70,499,183 lunar months, based on an assumed mean lunar month of 29 days 12 hours 44 minutes 2
           <span class="mwe-math-element">
            <span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">
             <math alttext="{\displaystyle {\tfrac {49928114}{70499183}}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
              <semantics>
               <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
                 <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                  <mstyle displaystyle="false" scriptlevel="0">
                   <mfrac>
                    <mn>
                     49928114
                    </mn>
                    <mn>
                     70499183
                    </mn>
                   </mfrac>
                  </mstyle>
                 </mrow>
                </mstyle>
               </mrow>
               <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">
                {\displaystyle {\tfrac {49928114}{70499183}}}
               </annotation>
              </semantics>
             </math>
            </span>
            <img alt="{\tfrac {49928114}{70499183}}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/8971e4cfa1e5ba9e2ab8a7dc3dae2c2a3b7b09e1" style="vertical-align: -1.338ex; width:7.412ex; height:3.843ex;"/>
           </span>
           seconds. (Seidelmann (1992), p. 582) [To properly function as an Easter
           <i>
            computus
           </i>
           , this lunisolar cycle must have the same mean year as the Gregorian solar cycle, and indeed that is exactly the case.]
          </span>
         </li>
         <li id="cite_note-16">
          <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
           <b>
            <a href="#cite_ref-16">
             ^
            </a>
           </b>
          </span>
          <span class="reference-text">
           The extreme length of the Gregorian Easter
           <i>
            computus
           </i>
           is due to its being the product of the 19-year Metonic cycle, the thirty different possible values of the epact, and the least common multiple (10,000) of the 400-year and 2,500-year solar and lunar correction cycles.
           <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15">
            <a href="#cite_note-15">
             [11]
            </a>
           </sup>
          </span>
         </li>
         <li id="cite_note-17">
          <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
           <b>
            <a href="#cite_ref-17">
             ^
            </a>
           </b>
          </span>
          <span class="reference-text">
           The same result is obtained by summing the fractional parts implied by the rule:
           <span class="nowrap">
            365 + ​
            <span class="frac nowrap">
             <sup>
              1
             </sup>
             ⁄
             <sub>
              4
             </sub>
            </span>
            − ​
            <span class="frac nowrap">
             <sup>
              1
             </sup>
             ⁄
             <sub>
              100
             </sub>
            </span>
            + ​
            <span class="frac nowrap">
             <sup>
              1
             </sup>
             ⁄
             <sub>
              400
             </sub>
            </span>
            = 365 + 0.25 − 0.01 + 0.0025 = 365.2425
           </span>
          </span>
         </li>
         <li id="cite_note-18">
          <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
           <b>
            <a href="#cite_ref-18">
             ^
            </a>
           </b>
          </span>
          <span class="reference-text">
           The last major Christian region to accept the Alexandrian rules was the
           <a href="/wiki/Carolingian_Empire" title="Carolingian Empire">
            Carolingian Empire
           </a>
           (most of Western Europe) during 780–800. The last monastery in England to accept the Alexandrian rules did so in 931, and a few churches in southwest Asia beyond the eastern border of the
           <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_Empire" title="Byzantine Empire">
            Byzantine Empire
           </a>
           continued to use rules that differed slightly, causing four dates for Easter to differ every 532 years.
          </span>
         </li>
         <li id="cite_note-33">
          <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
           <b>
            <a href="#cite_ref-33">
             ^
            </a>
           </b>
          </span>
          <span class="reference-text">
           1919 in the regions comprising the former Kingdoms of Serbia and Montenegro (present-day Kosovo, Montenegro, Serbia and Macedonia). The western and northern regions of what became Yugoslavia were already using the Gregorian calendar. For example, most of Slovenia adopted the Gregorian calendar at the same time as Austria in 1583. Coastal Croatia, which was at the time ruled by Venice, adopted the Gregorian calendar in 1582. Inland Croatia, ruled by the Habsburgs, adopted it in 1587 along with Hungary. The Gregorian calendar was used in Bosnia and Herzegovina since the 16th century by the Catholic population and was formally adopted for government use in 1878 following occupation by Austria-Hungary.
          </span>
         </li>
         <li id="cite_note-54">
          <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
           <b>
            <a href="#cite_ref-54">
             ^
            </a>
           </b>
          </span>
          <span class="reference-text">
           In Scotland the legal start of year had been moved to 1 January in 1600 (Mike Spathaky.
           <a class="external text" href="http://www.cree.name/genuki/dates.htm" rel="nofollow">
            Old Style New Style dates and the change to the Gregorian calendar
           </a>
           ).
          </span>
         </li>
        </ol>
       </div>

        <li id="cite_note-53">
         <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
          <b>
           <a href="#cite_ref-53">
            ^
           </a>
          </b>
         </span>
         <span class="reference-text">
          Benjamin Woolley,
          <i>
           The Queen's Conjurer: The science and magic of Dr. John Dee, adviser to Queen Elizabeth I
          </i>
          (New York: Henry Holt, 2001) p. 173
         </span>
        </li>
        <li id="cite_note-55">
         <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
          <b>
           <a href="#cite_ref-55">
            ^
           </a>
          </b>
         </span>
         <span class="reference-text">
          Spathaky, Mike
          <a class="external text" href="http://www.cree.name/genuki/dates.htm" rel="nofollow">
           Old Style New Style dates and the change to the Gregorian calendar
          </a>
          . "increasingly parish registers, in addition to a new year heading after 24th March showing, for example '1733', had another heading at the end of the following December indicating '1733/4'. This showed where the New Style 1734 started even though the Old Style 1733 continued until 24th March. ... We as historians have no excuse for creating ambiguity and must keep to the notation described above in one of its forms. It is no good writing simply 20th January 1745, for a reader is left wondering whether we have used the Old or the New Style reckoning. The date should either be written 20th January 1745 OS (if indeed it was Old Style) or as 20th January 1745/6. The hyphen (1745-6) is best avoided as it can be interpreted as indicating a period of time."
         </span>
        </li>
        <li id="cite_note-56">
         <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
          <b>
           <a href="#cite_ref-56">
            ^
           </a>
          </b>
         </span>
         <span class="reference-text">
          <a class="external text" href="http://www.britannica.com/eb/article-38556/Russia:" rel="nofollow">
           The October (November) Revolution
          </a>
          Britannica encyclopaedia, A demonstration of New Style meaning the Gregorian calendar.
         </span>
        </li>
        <li id="cite_note-57">
         <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
          <b>
           <a href="#cite_ref-57">
            ^
           </a>
          </b>
         </span>
         <span class="reference-text">
          Stockton, J.R.
          <a class="external text" href="http://www.merlyn.demon.co.uk/miscdate.htm#Styles" rel="nofollow">
           Date Miscellany I: The Old and New Styles
          </a>
          "The terms 'Old Style' and 'New Style' are now commonly used for both the 'Start of Year' and 'Leap Year' [(Gregorian calendar)] changes (England &amp; Wales: both in 1752; Scotland: 1600, 1752). I believe that, properly and historically, the 'Styles' really refer only to the 'Start of Year' change (from March 25th to January 1st); and that the 'Leap Year' change should be described as the change from Julian to Gregorian."
         </span>
        </li>

         <span class="reference-text">
          It's not unusual for month names to be based on natural descriptions but this etymology is sometimes doubted since no other
          <i>
           Roman
          </i>
          months have such names.

         <span class="reference-text">

          <a href="/wiki/Plutarch" title="Plutarch">
           Plutarch
          </a>
          <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64">
           <a href="#cite_note-64">
            [54]
           </a>

          <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Varro" title="Varro">
           Varro
          </a>
          and
          <a href="/wiki/Cincius" title="Cincius">
           Cincius
          </a>
          .
